Preface
This master thesis was submitted at the Department of Mechanical Engineering at
the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) as a ΎυΒnal exam to obtain a Master of
Science degree in Materials and Manufacturing Engineering. The workload of this
thesis corresponds to 35 ECTS, with a project duration from January 28th 2019
to August 18th 2019. The project has been carried out at the Section of Materials
and Surface Engineering at DTU under the supervision of Professor Marcel A. J.
Somers and Associate Professor Thomas L. Christiansen as well as Philipp Schuler
and Arnulf H├╢rtnagl from the Institut f├╝r WerkstoΎυΑsystemtechnik Thurgau an der
Hochschule Konstanz (WITg).
Kongens Lyngby, August 18th 2019, . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

Abstract
Composition Residual Stress and Magnetic
Properties in Carburized Austenitic Stainless Steel
Konstantin Victor Werner
Technical University of Denmark
Low temperature surface hardening has proven to extend the applicability of austenitic
stainless steels by improving their low wear and fatigue resistance, while maintain-
ing or even improving the corrosion resistance. Research has shown that the uptake
of nitrogen depends on the amount of nitride forming elements and will eventually
result in partly plastic accommodation of the residual stress by lattice rotation as
well as cause a transition from para- to ferromagnetism. Similar ΎυΒndings have so
far not been reported for low temperature carburized austenitic stainless steel. This
project aims therefore to determine the inΎυΓuence of the chromium and nickel equiv-
alent of austenitic stainless steels on low temperature carburizing with regards to
the chemical composition, the residual stress and the magnetic properties in carbon
expanded austenite.
The microstructure of the surface hardened austenitic stainless steels was investi-
gated with light optical microscopy, electron backscatter diΎυΑraction, X-ray diΎυΑrac-
tion and glow discharged optical emission spectroscopy to describe the morphology,
residual stress, lattice expansion and composition in carbon expanded austenite.
Besides, the magnetic properties were characterized by magnetic force microscopy
and FERITSCOPE measurements.
The results show unambiguously that the carbon uptake, and as a consequence
thereof the residual stress, in low temperature carburized austenitic stainless steel
depend signiΎυΒcantly on the alloysέΑβ chromium equivalent. Higher chromium equiva-
lents promote the solubility of carbon in expanded austenite and lead to an increase
in residual stress, causing partly plastic deformation, which is accommodated by
lattice rotation. Furthermore, the results from magnetic force microscopy and the
FERITSCOPE RέΔζmeasurements imply that low temperature carburized austenitic
stainless steel can become magnetic without forming ferrite or martensite.
The
magnetic domains are restricted to the upper part of the expanded austenite zone
and arise from the combination of a high Ni-equivalent and carbon-induced lattice
expansion (>14.5 at%), whereby the Ni-equivalent appears to be dominating.

Reading Guide
Chapter 1 is the initial chapter of this thesis and introduces the concept of ex-
panded austenite brieΎυΓy. Furthermore, the projectέΑβs motivation as well as its scope
are described.
Chapter 2 elucidates the scientiΎυΒc background of the formation of expanded austen-
ite. Furthermore, this chapter gives a state of the art review of stress measurements
in surface layers, especially expanded austenite, and the occurrence of a transition
from para- to ferromagnetism in expanded austenite.
Chapter 3 is giving a detailed description of the experimental procedures, com-
prising information concerning material related data, such as chemical composition,
specimen preparation and experimental setups of the various characterization tech-
niques.
Chapter 4 presents the results obtained by LOM, EBSD, GDOES and XRD and
establishes a connection between results obtained with the diΎυΑerent characterization
techniques.
Chapter 5 discusses the obtained results in the context of the current state of
knowledge and assesses the resultsέΑβ plausibility.
Chapter 6 provides a short summary of the projectέΑβs most important ΎυΒndings.

1 Introduction
Stainless steels constitute a group of iron based alloys with a chromium content
of at least 10.5 wt% and a maximum carbon content of 1.2 wt% [1]. Depending
on the actual chemical composition, stainless steels can feature either an austenitic,
ferritic, martensitic or an austenitic-ferritic duplex microstructure. Austenitic stain-
less steels are the most common type of stainless steel and contain at least 16 wt%
of chromium as well as nickel, manganese, nitrogen and carbon to stabilize the face
centered cubic (fcc) austenite [2]. Due to their outstanding corrosion resistance
and high ductility as well as toughness within a wide temperature range, austenitic
stainless steels are widely used in the chemical, petrochemical and food industry.
As a consequence of the relatively low hardness, however, austenitic stainless steels
suΎυΑer from extensive wear in tribological contacts, particularly galling and fretting,
and limited fatigue resistance.
Low temperature surface hardening (LTSH) by dissolving carbon and/or nitrogen
in austenite has proven to be very eΎυΑective in improving the wear resistance of
austenitic stainless steel [3έΑΥ5], as the corrosion resistance is not impaired but can
even be enhanced [3,6,7]. The formed layer of expanded austenite opens up a wide
range of new applications for austenitic stainless steels that require a combination
of high corrosion and wear resistance. Low temperature carburizing (LTC) and
low temperature nitriding (LTN) can be performed by thermochemical treatments
either in plasma, by implantation techniques or as gaseous processes [3, 8]. The
formation of the respective carbides/nitrides is prevented by introducing nitrogen
or carbon at temperatures below 723 K and 823 K, respectively [9έΑΥ12].
The dissolution of vast amounts of carbon and/or nitrogen leads to the formation
of compressive residual stresses of up to several GPaέΑβs within the expanded austen-
ite zone [13έΑΥ17]. Eventually, the built up stresses exceed the yield strength of the
material and are partly accommodated by plastic deformation [18έΑΥ21]. This, as
well as the transition from para- to ferromagnetism [18, 22έΑΥ25], has so far only
been observed for nitrogen expanded austenite. The vast majority of publications
concerning residual stress and magnetism in expanded austenite is focused on the
common austenitic stainless steel grades AISI 316/316L and disregards eΎυΑects of
the stainless steelέΑβs chemical composition.
The aim of this Master thesis is therefore to investigate the inΎυΓuence of the base
materialέΑβs chemical composition on low temperature carburizing with respect to
the composition, built up residual stresses and an eventual transition from para- to
ferromagnetism within the layer of carbon expanded austenite. Insights and results
from this project could eventually be used in the development of austenitic stainless
steels that are tailored to be low temperature surface hardened.
1
==PAGE:28==
==PAGE:29==
2 Theoretical Background
2.1 Concept of Low Temperature Surface
Hardening and Expanded Austenite
The corrosion resistance of stainless steels is based on the formation of a thin, but
stable layer of chromium (hydro)oxide on the steelέΑβs surface. To maintain the cor-
rosion resistance, this layer has to be reformed upon damage. Therefore, chromium
has to be present in solid solution and thus be able to diΎυΑuse to the surface and
replenish the layer of chromium (hydro)oxide. Traditional gaseous heat treatments,
like carburizing and nitriding, could improve the inherently low wear resistance
of austenitic stainless steel and therefore expand their ΎυΒeld of applications. The
normally applied treatment temperatures above 773 K (for nitriding) and 1000
K (for carburizing) would, however, lead to the formation of the respective car-
bides/nitrides and hence impair the corrosion resistance by lowering the amount of
chromium available in solid solution [26].
By nitriding at temperatures of >1250 K, the development of chromium nitride can
be circumvented, since the nitrides are no longer thermodynamically stable. The
process is referred to as high temperature solution nitriding (HTSN) and requires,
apart from high temperatures, a N2 pressure, usually less than 1 bar, to dissolve
nitrogen. On the contrary, high temperature solution carburizing (HTSC) is practi-
cally unfeasible, since the solubility of carbon in austenite at such high temperatures
is lower than that of nitrogen. Besides, the compositional and temperature range
is narrower, and the cooling rate is even more critical than for HTSN [21].
As shown in the schematic time temperature transformation (TTT) diagram in Fig-
ure 2.1a, the formation of chromium carbides/nitrides can otherwise be kinetically
impeded by nitriding or carburizing at temperatures below 723 K and έΚΙ823 K, re-
spectively [9,11,12]. Compared to the diΎυΑusion of interstitial elements like carbon
and nitrogen, chromium diΎυΑuses slowly at these temperatures. This results in the
formation of a layer of so-called carbon (╬│C) or nitrogen (╬│N) expanded austenite,
which can reach thicknesses of approximately 20-30 ┬╡m without forming the respec-
tive chromium-based compounds.
3
==PAGE:30==
2 Theoretical Background
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.1: (a) Schematic TTT diagram for austenite containing carbon/nitrogen,
formed at either high temperatures or low temperatures so that the
formation of the respective carbides/nitrides is prevented (compare to
[21]), (b) Light optical micrograph of low temperature gas carburized
AISI 316 at T=780 K, t=6 h, KC=έΙηin pure CO [8].
Expanded austenite appears in light optical micrographs as a nearly featureless,
bright layer at the materialέΑβs surface, as shown exemplarily in Figure 2.1b. Grain
boundaries of the underlying austenite continue in the layer of expanded austenite,
indicating that expanded austenite is indeed solely a supersaturated solid solution
of carbon and/or nitrogen and not a new phase, as the formerly used designation
"S-phase" implies [19]. The bright appearance in light optical microscopy (LOM)
micrographs is related to the higher corrosion potential after low temperature sur-
face hardening [27].
The improved wear resistance of expanded austenite stems from the enormous in-
crease in hardness, up to 1000 HV in case of ╬│C and up to 1500 HV for ╬│N, resulting
from the uptake of colossal amounts of carbon and nitrogen [8,28]. The diΎυΑerence in
surface hardness of carbon and nitrogen expanded austenite is a result of the lower
solubility of C in expanded austenite, see hereto also section 2.2. While the hard-
ness within carbon expanded austenite decreases gradually until the bulk hardness
level is reached, low temperature nitrided stainless steel exhibits a sharp drop in
hardness from a plateau in ╬│N to bulk hardness, within a few ┬╡m around the ╬│NέΙΤ╬│
transition.
4
==PAGE:31==
2 Theoretical Background
Figure 2.2: Exemplary hardness proΎυΒles of AISI 316 carburized (T=773 K, t=4 h,
KC=έΙη, nitrided (T=713 K, t=18.5 h, KN=έΙη) as well as nitrocarburized
(carburized and subsequently nitrided with the given parameters) [8].
The often reported enhanced fatigue properties of low temperature surface hardened
austenitic stainless steels relate to the compressive residual stress gradients, in the
magnitude of several GPa, that arise along with the hardness proΎυΒles in expanded
austenite as a consequence of the ingress of carbon and or nitrogen.
2.2 Compositional Gradient and Lattice Expansion
in Expanded Austenite
The ingress of carbon and/or nitrogen, residing on the octahedral sites of the fcc
austenite, leads to a lattice expansion which is related to the concentration and
the type of interstitially dissolved atoms [29]. Typically observed concentrations of
nitrogen in ╬│N range from 11.5-38 at % [19,25]. Contrary to ╬│N, carbon expanded
austenite exhibits a maximum solubility of έΚΙ17 at% [30], which is not always at-
tained experimentally. Exceeding this maximum solubility results in the precipita-
tion of the so called H├νgg carbide (M5C2) [30, 31]. The diΎυΑerence in solubility of
N and C in expanded austenite attributes to the strength of their interaction with
chromium, whereby Cr and N interact stronger with each other than Cr and C [8].
Furthermore, it is suggested that higher contents of strong nitride forming elements,
such as Cr [8, 32] as well as e.g. V, Nb [32] and Ti [33], enhance the solubility of
nitrogen in expanded austenite. As far as the author is aware, corresponding re-
sults have not yet been reported for carbon expanded austenite, although this is
considered likely to be the case.
As mentioned in Section 2.4.3 the carbon or nitrogen content of expanded austenite
can be related to the strain-free lattice parameter (dhkl
╬╡0 ). Results for stress free
nitrided and carburized thin foils of AISI 316 are shown in Figures 2.3a and 2.3b.
The determined lattice expansion coeΎυΔcients, in terms of the occupancy (yN &
yC), for ╬│N (0.059724 nm/yN) and ╬│C (0.06029 nm/yC) are identical within the
experimental accuracy [19, 34].
Furthermore, Figure 2.3a shows that the lattice
5
==PAGE:32==
2 Theoretical Background
parameter of ╬│έΑ▓-Fe4N lies on the regression line for ╬│N. Hence, the structure of ╬│N
with a nitrogen content of 20 at% can be approximated by the crystal structure
of the stoichiometric ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4N. On the other hand, the lattice parameter of non-
expanded austenite does not match with the value predicted by the regression line.
This will further be addressed in the subsequent section.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.3: (a) Lattice expansion of homogeneous nitrogen expanded austenite of
gas nitrided AISI 316 thin foils at 718 K, determined by XRD [19],
(b) Lattice expansion of homogeneous carbon expanded austenite, gas
carburized AISI 316 thin foils at 722 K, determined by XRD [34].
Long range ordering (LRO) of nitrogen atoms in expanded austenite has been ob-
served by selected area diΎυΑraction (SAED) [35], while recent experiments did also
prove the existence of LRO in nitrogen expanded austenite by XRD for yN = 0.55,
likely being associated with the presence of ╬│έΑ▓-Fe4N [25]. On the contrary, LRO
has so far not been observed for carbon expanded austenite, although ╬│έΙΤFe4C is,
according to density functional theory calculations, predicted to be stable [36,37].
Calculated lattice parameters for ╬│έΙΤFe4C are in the range of 3.740 - 3.768 ├Ζ [36,38].
This corresponds to an extrapolation of the line in Figure 2.3b to ╬│C = 0.25.
2.3 Transition from Para- to Ferromagnetism in
Expanded Austenite
In general, austenitic stainless steels are considered to be paramagnetic at room
temperature. Density functional theory calculations have however shown that the
magnetic properties of fcc austenite can, depending on the distance between the iron
atoms, change from para- to ferromagnetism [39, 40], whereby larger interatomic
distances favour the latter one. Ferromagnetism in nitrogen expanded austenite
has been reported to occur conΎυΒned in a near surface layer (Figure 2.4a) [18,22,25].
The transition from para- to ferromagnetism is primarily attributed to the lattice
expansion, caused by the dissolution of vast amounts of nitrogen. Partitioning and
enrichment of inherently magnetic nickel and iron as well as the similarity between
the structure of ╬│N and ╬│έΑ▓-Fe4N are regarded as further reasons for the observed
magnetism [24, 41]. As shown in Figure 2.4b the change in magnetic properties
arises at a nitrogen content of approximately 14 at% (yN = 0.17) [22,23,25]. This
6
==PAGE:33==
2 Theoretical Background
threshold for the transition from para-ferromagnetism explains furthermore, why
ferromagnetic carbon expanded austenite with its limited carbon solubility has so
far not been observed.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.4: (a) Magnetic force microscopy image of nitrided AISI 316, indicating the
presence of ferro- and paramagnetic expanded austenite [22], (b) Lat-
tice expansion of homogeneous carbon and nitrogen expanded austenite
versus interstitial content, with linear relations determined by [19, 34].
Para- and ferromagnetic regions indicated by zigzag lines [25].
The lattice parameter for ferro- and paramagnetic expanded austenite at yC/N =
0.17 diΎυΑers by 1.1 %, according to Brink et. al., caused by spontaneous volume mag-
netostriction [25]. This could explain, why the lattice parameter of the untreated
steel in Figure 2.3a is shifted to a lower value than the one predicted by the given
regression line.
2.4 Residual Stress and Residual Stress Analysis
by X-ray DiΎυΑraction
Residual stresses within materials arise due to processing, e.g.
heat treatment,
machining or welding, as a result of incoherent elastic and/or plastic deformation.
Depending on the length scale they are acting on in a material, residual stresses are
categorized as tresses of 1st, 2nd and 3rd kind, see also Figure 2.5a. The residual
stresses of 1st (╧ΔI) kind are commonly denoted as macro-stresses and represent the
volumetric average of all position-dependent stress states within a considered mate-
rial volume. The diΎυΑerence between the stress within a grain and ╧ΔI is described by
the residual stresses of 2nd (╧ΔII) kind. Deviations of the residual stress level within
a single grain are stresses of 3rd (╧ΔIII) kind. Together with those of second-kind,
they are labelled as microstresses.
7

(b)

ing the diΎυΑerent kinds of residual stress [42, p. 102], (b) Stresses and
their direction for a homogeneously loaded unit cube at static equilib-
rium [43, p. 16].

normal strains.

2.4.2 Quasi-isotropic Materials
In the previous chapter, it was assumed that the stress-strain relation for a given
material is not direction-dependent (isotropic) and that stresses and strains can
therefore be related to each other by YoungέΑβs modulus and PoissonέΑβs ratio. Most
metallic materials are however anisotropic and can only be considered as quasi-
isotropic in a texture-less polycrystalline state.
In case of an anisotropic single
crystalline material, the stress-strain relation is direction-dependent and HookeέΑβs

2 Theoretical Background
Contrary to the stress-strain relation of a single crystalline material which can
be described based on the stiΎυΑness or compliance vector, polycrystalline materials
constitute of a multitude of inherently anisotropic grains. The macroscopic relation
between stress and strain along a certain direction in space is hence given by the
weighted average of each single grainέΑβs elastic constants along this direction. The
calculation of these average elastic constants, also X-ray elastic constants (XECs),
is based on so called grain interaction models (GIMs).
Voigt - Grain Interaction Model
The grain interaction model by Voigt assumes that all grains in a polycrystalline
material are strained equally. The XECs for the Voigt model do not depend on the
crystallographic plane and are calculated as [43,44]:

2.4.4 Information Depth and DiΎυΑraction Geometry
The penetration of X-rays used for XRD of structural materials, e.g. steel, is typ-
ically within the range of several micrometers.
Lattice spacingέΑβs determined by
X-ray diΎυΑraction are hence an intensity-weighted average over the so called infor-
mation depth ╧Ε. In case of a symmetric diΎυΑraction geometry, ╧Ε depends strongly on
the tilt angle ╧Ι and the Bragg angle ╬α. This poses a problem for low temperature
surface hardened stainless steels, exhibiting a steep stress as well as a composition
gradient in the expanded austenite layer. As a result of the information depthέΑβs de-
pendence on ╬α and ╧Ι, the corresponding sin2╧Ι-plots will be nonlinear (Figure 2.7).
This results in an erroneous stress state, if the variation in information depth is
not taken into account during the stress determination. Stresses arising solely from
this dependency, also called ghost stresses, were reported to be as high as several
hundreds of MPa. Several methods were suggested in the past to unravel the actual
stress state and avoid ghost stresses [19]. A comprehensive synopsis on stress mea-
surements in expanded austenite, addressing the issues of ghost stresses, is given by
Somers et al. [21].
Performing XRD measurements with an asymmetric diΎυΑraction geometry, by ap-
plying a ΎυΒxed gracing incidence angle, reduces the information depthέΑβs dependence
on ╬α. Within a relatively narrow 2╬α range, as shown in Figure 2.8a, ╧Ε is basically
only dependent on ╧Ι. Nevertheless, nonlinear sin2╧Ι dependencies as a result of the
varying information depth were reported, resulting in ghost stresses of up to 350
MPa [47].
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.8: (a) Information depth in case of an asymmetric diΎυΑraction geometry,
applying a ΎυΒxed gracing incident angle of ╬▒G = 2έΩο, as a function of
sin2╧Ι for Bragg angles of 53 and 83έΩο2╬α [15], (b) Constant penetration
depth ╧Ε = 0.5 ┬╡m, realized by adjusting ╧Ι and ╧Θ angle for 2╬α = 62έΩο[21].
The penetration depth for an asymmetric diΎυΑraction geometry can be calculated
according to Equation (2.4.37) [48].

constant penetration depth for varying ╧Ι angles can be achieved by tailoring of the
gracing incidence angle ╬▒G and the ╧ΘέΙΤangle, see also Figure 2.8b. The construc-
tion of a sin2╧ΙέΙΤplot based on measurements acquired with a constant penetration

gradients. Due to the implicit character of Equation (2.4.37), the determination
of ╬▒G/╧Θ pairs, so that ╧Ε is constant, has to be performed either numerically or
graphically [21, 49, 50]. It should be noted that the probed lattice planes in graz-

surface normal as a consequence of the applied grazing incidence angle, even when

2.4.5 Rotation of the DiΎυΑraction Vector
As a consequence of the variation of the grazing incidence angle (╬▒G) and the
╧ΘέΙΤangle, in order to keep the penetration depth constant or at least limit its
variation, the diΎυΑraction vector rotates around the diΎυΑractometerέΑβs ╧ΧέΙΤaxis [48].
For an ideal rotationally symmetric stress state this will have no inΎυΓuence on the
sin2╧ΙέΙΤplot, since the lattice planes probed along the rotating diΎυΑraction vector are
subjected to an equal magnitude of stress. In case of a non-rotationally symmet-
ric stress state, the stress state for a set of lattice planes depends hence on the
orientation of the diΎυΑraction vector with regards to the specimens principal stress
directions. The lattice spacings for the diΎυΑerent combinations of ╬▒G and ╧Θ in a
sin2╧ΙέΙΤplot correspond thus to diΎυΑering stress magnitudes, which causes eventually
curved dhkl vs. sin2 ╧Ι relations. To compensate for the rotation of the diΎυΑraction
vector around the ╧ΧέΙΤaxis, Erbacher et al. suggested to rotate the specimen around
╧Χ by an equal magnitude, but in opposite direction [49], calculated according to:
έΙΗ╧Χ = έΙΤarccos(tan(╬α έΙΤ╬▒G)
sin ╧Θ
)
(2.4.39)
2.4.6 General Guidelines for Residual Stress Analysis by XRD
Choice of Lattice Plane
It is generally recommended to choose diΎυΑraction peaks located at high 2╬α angles
(2╬α > 125έΩο) [51]. Due to the non-linear relation between lattice spacing and 2╬α
(Equation (2.4.4)), variations in the peak position (έΙΗ╬α) caused by misalignment
or peak ΎυΒtting, will inΎυΓuence the lattice spacing less in case of high angular peaks.
However, peaks located at high 2╬α values do often exhibit lower intensity and are
wider than low angular peaks.
έΙΗdhkl
dhkl
= έΙΤέΙΗ╬α cot(╬α)
(2.4.40)
16
==PAGE:43==
2 Theoretical Background
Determining the stress state for high multiplicity peaks (number of equivalent
planes) may reduce the oscillations in the sin2╧Ι-plot of textured materials [43,51].
Furthermore, the peak intensity should be high enough (I > 1000 Counts) in order
to enable a proper peak ΎυΒt [51].
Large Grained and Textured Specimens
Specimens featuring large grains and a pronounced texture can beneΎυΒt from an
oscillation along or around one of the diΎυΑractometerέΑβs axis whilst measuring. Thus,
a larger quantity of grains will fulΎυΒll BraggέΑβs condition, which results in a more
deΎυΒned diΎυΑraction peak due to enhanced grain statistics [51].
Determination of the Peak Position
Peak positions for residual stress measurements are determined by ΎυΒtting the mea-
sured intensity distribution with a mathematical function and given either by the
curves apex or itέΑβs centre of gravity. Commonly used functions for ΎυΒtting symmet-
ric intensity distributions are Lorentzian, Pearson VII, Gaussian and Pseudo-Voigt.
Asymmetric intensity distributions resulting from steep stress or compositional gra-
dients, cannot be ΎυΒtted with one of the above mentioned functions. However, non-
symmetric peaks can be described e.g by a parabola, ΎυΒtted to the top 15 % of the
intensity distribution [43].
2.5 Compressive Residual Stresses and their
Plastic Accommodation in Expanded
Austenite
As mentioned in Section 2.2, the dissolution of huge amounts of carbon and/or nitro-
gen in austenite causes a lattice expansion up to several percent, see Figures 2.3a
and 2.3b. As a result of the lattice expansion which is, as shown in Figure 2.9,
constrained in the plane by the substrate, enormous in plane compressive residual
stresses in the order of several GPa are built up in the layer of expanded austenite.
The magnitude of the compressive residual stress decreases throughout the layer
of expanded austenite and is balanced by a tensile stress state in the underlying
austenite. The magnitude of the tensile stresses is, as a consequence of the necessary
equilibrium of forces and moments, far lower than the compressive residual stress.
17
==PAGE:44==
2 Theoretical Background
Figure 2.9: Schematic description of the residual compressive stress state within
expanded austenite and an eventual out of plane plastic accommodation.
2.5.1 Compressive Residual Stress in Carbon and Nitrogen
Expanded Austenite
Compressive residual stresses in the range of 2.0 έΙΤ2.7 GPa were reported for AISI
316/316L upon low-temperature carburizing [13, 16, 17]. Residual stresses deter-
mined by nano-indentation and X-ray diΎυΑraction, after reconstruction of the lattice
spacing proΎυΒles, are in accordance (Figure 2.10c) [17].
The stresses determined
by Cristiansen and Somers are likely to be overestimated due to an inappropriate
choice of the lattice plane (d200
╬│C ) [47], see hereto also Section 2.5.3. The stress and
composition proΎυΒle (Figures 2.10a and 2.10c) in the expanded austenite layer are
closely related to each other.
Given that the process parameters are comparable, residual stresses in nitrogen
expanded austenite are in general higher than in carbon expanded austenite as a
consequence of the higher content of interstitials (Section 2.2). Similar to the case
of carbon expanded austenite, stresses determined by probing the ╬│N200έΙΤplane and
reported to be as high as 7.5 GPa [29], are considered to be overestimated, as also
indicated by the results of Fernandes et al. in Figure 2.10d [47].
Results obtained by GI-XRD at a constant penetration depth of 0.5 ┬╡m indicate
the presence of compressive residual stresses as high as 5 GPa in low temperature
nitrided AISI 316L [21, 50, 52]. Simulations of low temperature surface hardened
austenitic stainless steels indicate the possibility of compressive residual stresses
reaching a magnitude of 4έΙΤ6 GPa [53,54]. Hence, the previously mentioned stresses
of about 5 GPa seem to be physically reasonable.

2.5.2 Plastic Accommodation of Residual Stress in Expanded
Austenite
Interstitial atoms being dissolved in austenitic stainless steel during low temperature
surface hardening contribute to a considerable amount to solid solution strength-
ening of expanded austenite [53, 55]. The residual stresses arising along with the
concentration proΎυΒle will eventually surpass the yield strength of expanded austen-
ite and result in plastic deformation, which will manifest itself by the occurrence of
slip lines [11,55], grain push out [19], dislocations and lattice rotation [20,21].
Measurements with electron backscatter diΎυΑraction (EBSD) conducted by Templier
et al.
indicate lattice rotations of up to 13έΩοin low temperature nitrided AISI
316L [20]. It was found that the grains rotate either towards <111> or <100>,
being the stable orientations of polycrystalline material under compression, whereas
the rotation to <111> is dominant. The magnitude by which a grain rotates, as
shown in Figures 2.11a and 2.11b, depends signiΎυΒcantly on its orientation [20,21].
19
==PAGE:46==
2 Theoretical Background
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.11: (a) Lattice rotation of low temperature nitrided AISI 316 at 713 K
for 8 h in NH3. Orientations in the inverse pole ΎυΒgures (bottom) are
given from white to black along the arrows in the image quality maps
(top) [21], (b) misorientation proΎυΒles of the grains along the arrows
indicated in the image quality maps [21].
As far as the author is aware, plastic deformation has, apart from the observation
of slip lines in ╬│C [55], only been reported for nitrogen expanded austenite.
2.5.3 Choice of Lattice Plane and X-ray Elastic Constants
As mentioned in Section 2.4.6, it is favourable to use diΎυΑraction peaks for the stress
analysis by XRD that are located at a high 2╬α position, since errors in the diΎυΑrac-
tometer alignment and the determination of the peak position do not inΎυΓuence the
results as much as in case of low 2╬α diΎυΑraction peaks. Since residual stresses in
low temperature surface hardened austenitic stainless steels should be probed with
Cr-radiation, solely the 111, 200 and 220 peaks can be used for stress analysis.
The 220 peak of expanded austenite exhibits often relatively low intensities, which
makes it unsuitable and limits the peak choice eΎυΑectively to the 111 and 200 peak.
As shown by Sun and Bell, the 200 peak of nitrogen expanded austenite is, com-
pared to the expected peak positions of an ideal face centered cubic lattice, shifted
to lower 2╬α values [11]. It is reasoned that this anomaly is caused by a change of
the eΎυΑective XECs due to plastic deformation, which is more pronounced for the
200 than for the 111 planes of expanded austenite [56]. Hence, residual stresses
determined by probing the 200 peak will be systematically higher than the stresses
determined from the 111 peak, as experimentally shown by Fernandes et al. [47].
It is hence recommended to use the 111 peak of expanded austenite instead of the
200 peak.
The inΎυΓuence of interstitial atoms, such as carbon and nitrogen, on the XECs of low
temperature surface hardened austenitic stain less is hitherto unknown. Therefore,
most publications rely on the XECs determined from single crystal elastic constants
measured by Teklu et. al for an austenitic Fe-18wt%Cr-12wt%Ni alloy [57].
20
==PAGE:47==
2 Theoretical Background
Findings by Tromas et al. show that the anisotropy of the elastic modulus (Ehkl) in
nitrogen expanded austenite (E200 > E111) is reversed compared to the anisotropy
in austenite (E111 > E200) and corresponds to the anisotropy of ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4N for a
nitrogen concentration of έΚΙ25 at% [58].

This chapter provides information with respect to the investigated materials, such
as the chemical composition and the low temperature surface hardening process, as
well as details of the experimental work that has been conducted during the project.

with a colloidal silica suspension, while the specimens for LOM, hardness testing
and glow discharge optical emission spectroscopy (GDOES) were solely ground to
23
==PAGE:50==
3 Experimental Procedure
1000 grit.
In addition to the low temperature surface hardened specimens, one
untreated specimen of each alloy was kept as a reference for XRD.
3.2 Light Optical Microscopy
Light optical microscopy was conducted on a Zeiss Axio Vert.A1 using polarized
light (NA = 0.75).
The sample preparation for LOM consisted of grinding on
silicon carbide paper from 500 grit up to 4000 grit on a Struers Rotopol-22 for 4
minutes on each grit size, while the force was reduced after 2 minutes from 10 N
to 5 N. Subsequent polishing was done with 3 ┬╡m, 1 ┬╡m and 0.25 ┬╡m diamond
suspension for 3:30 minutes, respectively. For the 1 ┬╡m and 0.25 ┬╡m step, the force
was kept constant at 5 N, whereas the force was reduced from 10 N to 5 N after
1:30 minutes in case of the 3 ┬╡m step. (Specimen holder and polishing cloth were
counter-rotating). Etching was performed by immersion directly after the 0.25 ┬╡m
polishing step in Aqua Regia (60 ml HCl, 20 ml HNO3 and 2 drops of Dr. Vogels
Sparbeize) for 15 - 45 seconds.
3.3 Hardness Measurements
Hardness depth proΎυΒles were measured by Vickers micro-indentation according to
ASTM E92-17 [60] on polished cross sections with a Future-Tech FM-700 diamond
indenter, equipped with an Ikegami ICD-828PRXACDC 1/2" 530 TVL camera.
The dwell time was set to 10 s and the test load was set to 5 g. The given hardness
values are averaged over three parallel indentation proΎυΒles. The spacing between
the indents was at least 2.5 times the indentsέΑβ diagonal. Hence, the ΎυΒrst indentation
below the specimensέΑβ surface was set at 7.5 ┬╡m.
3.4 Glow Discharge Optical Emission Spectroscopy
Analysis of the chemical composition throughout the expanded austenite zone was
conducted by GDOES using a Horiba Jobin Yvon GD proΎυΒler 2.
The plasma
for sputtering was generated at a power of 40 W and a pressure of 1000 Pa.
The recorded intensity/chemical composition over time proΎυΒles were converted into
depth resolved chemical composition proΎυΒles by replacing the time with the mea-
sured crater depth, assuming a constant sputtering rate within the expanded austen-
ite. Crater depths were measured with an Alicona InΎυΒniteFocus R
έΔζoptical microscope
(NA = 0.20). Calibration for the substitutional elements was performed with a set
of austenitic stainless steel reference standards, while a layer of Cr23C6 formed on a
piece of tool steel (Sleipner R
έΔζby Uddeholm) was used as a calibration standard for
carbon. Details concerning the standards used for calibration are given in Table A.3
in the appendix.

==PAGE:51==
3 Experimental Procedure
3.5 X-ray DiΎυΑraction
X-ray diΎυΑraction was applied for phase analysis as well as to obtain lattice-spacing
and stress depth proΎυΒles. All measurements were conducted with a Bruker D8 AXS
X-ray diΎυΑractometer equipped with Cr-radiation (╬╗CrK╬▒1=2.2897 ├Ζ) and were op-
erated in point focus at a voltage of 35 kV and a current of 50 mA. The used optics
comprise a poly capillar on the side of the beam incident and an equatorial soller
as well as a 0.02 mm thick vanadium window on the detector side to remove the
CrK╬▓ radiation.
Depth proΎυΒling through the layer of expanded austenite was performed by con-
secutive removal of approximately 3 ┬╡m thick layers by manually polishing with
3 ┬╡m and 1 ┬╡m diamond suspension and ΎυΒnishing with a colloidal silica suspen-
sion (OP-S). The thickness of the removed layers was determined by tracing the
diagonals (Dx/y) of four hardness indents alongside polishing and calculating the
change in depth, corresponding to the thickness of the removed layer, according to
Equation (3.5.1). The hardness indentέΑβs diagonals were measured on a Zeiss Axio
Scope.A1 and averaged over ΎυΒve measurements. The removed depth έΙΗh follows
from the change in the indentέΑβs diagonal, DeltaDx/y:
έΙΗh =
έΙΗDx/y

έΙγ
2 tan(68έΩο)
(3.5.1)
The indents were spaced by at least 7 mm from the specimens edges to minimize
the risk of impairing the determination of the removed layerέΑβs thickness due to edge
rounding. This process was repeated consecutively after recording scans for phase
and stress analysis by XRD at the corresponding depth in the expanded austenite
layer.
3.5.1 X-ray DiΎυΑraction for Phase Analysis
Measurements for phase analysis were conducted in the range from 58 έΙΤ83έΩο2╬α,
including the 111 as well as the 200 peak of austenite and carbon expanded austenite,
using a step size of 0.04έΩο2╬α and a counting time of 5 seconds. In order to detect an
eventual formation of carbides in the outer layers of the expanded austenite zone,
the measurements were performed in an asymmetric diΎυΑraction geometry. Applying
a grazing incidence angle (╬▒G) of 3έΩοand a ╧ΘέΙΤangle of 10.4έΩοlimited the information
depth to 0.5 ┬╡m. The diΎυΑractograms were analyzed using BrukerέΑβs DIFFRAC.EVA
software.
3.5.2 X-ray DiΎυΑraction for Stress Analysis
DiΎυΑractograms for stress analysis and determination of the strain-free lattice pa-
rameter were recorded at a constant information depth of 0.5 ┬╡m, using a set of
six combinations of ╬▒G/╧Θ : 3έΩο/10.4έΩο, 3.3έΩο/25.3έΩο, 4έΩο/40έΩο, 5έΩο/50.2έΩο, 7έΩο/60.3έΩοand
12έΩο/68.5έΩο, as suggested by Somers et al. [21]. The measurements were performed
in a range from 58 έΙΤ72έΩο2╬α, covering the 111 peak of both austenite and expanded
25
==PAGE:52==
3 Experimental Procedure
austenite, at a counting time of 6 seconds and a step size of 0.03έΩο2╬α. Prior to
ΎυΒtting the peaks in OriginPro with a Pseudo-Voigt function or a parabola, for
asymmetric peaks, the intensity resulting from ╬╗CrK╬▒2 was subtracted in BrukerέΑβs
DIFFRAC.EVA software, assuming a 2:1 ratio of ╬╗CrK╬▒1 and ╬╗CrK╬▒2. In order to
minimize the eΎυΑects of large grains and texture possibly resulting from hot rolling,
the specimens were oscillated along the diΎυΑractometers y-axis at a speed of 1.5
mm/s during the measurements.
3.6 Electron Backscatter DiΎυΑraction
EBSD was carried out with a Gemini Supra 35 ΎυΒeld emission gun scanning electron
microscope (FEG-SEM) by Carl Zeiss. The scanning electron microscope (SEM) is
equipped with a Nordlys detector from Oxford Instruments and was operated at 15
kV, using an aperture size of 60 ┬╡m and tilting the stage by 70έΩο. The working dis-
tance (WD) was set to 13 mm. DiΎυΑraction patterns were acquired in an 500x100 ┬╡m
area, covering the expanded austenite zone as well as the substrate, with a step size
of 0.5 ┬╡m, and were analyzed with HKLέΑβs Flamenco software. All EBSD specimens
were electroplated with nickel to prevent edge rounding and were manually ground
and polished after being glued on a polishing jig, consisting of a steel cylinder being
inserted in the respective hollow-cylinder counterpart. A schematic depiction of the
jig is shown in Figure 3.1. Details concerning the exact procedure of the specimen
preparation are given in Table A.1 in the appendix.
Figure 3.1: Polishing jig used for manual specimen preparation for EBSD.
3.7 ProΎυΒle Measurements
Surface proΎυΒles were acquired with a Taylor Hobson Form Talysurf equipped with
a 2 ╬╝m measurement tip at at sampling speed of 0.30 mm/s. The measurements
were evenly spaced across the surface, 5 for specimen 2 and 4 for specimens 4 and 6
respectively, and performed perpendicular to the specimensέΑβ long edge. The present
proΎυΒles represent are averaged over all measurements and levelled using a 1storder
ΎυΒlter in SPIPTM.

4 Results and Interpretation
4.1 Metallography
Light optical micrographs of the low temperature carburized specimens are given
in Figures 4.1a to 4.1c.
It is evident that the grain size diΎυΑers from specimen
to specimen, with specimen 4 featuring the largest grains followed by specimen 6
and specimen 2. Contrary to a more gradual case-core transition in specimen 2,
specimen 4 and 6 exhibit a pronounced transition from ╬│C to ╬│. The bottom of
the expanded austenite layer of specimen 6 (Figure 4.1c) appears brighter than the
rest of the case. This is likely the result of an accumulation of the nitrogen already
dissolved in the material (Table 3.1), as it is displaced by the growing layer of carbon
expanded austenite.

==PAGE:54==
4 Results and Interpretation
It can furthermore be observed that grain boundaries from the austenite continue
in the zone of expanded austenite, as shown in Figure 4.1d. Case depths, as de-
termined from the micrographs, are 30 ┬▒ 1 ┬╡m for specimen 2, 33 ┬▒ 1 ┬╡m for
specimen 4 and 39 ┬▒ 1 ┬╡m for specimen 6 and seem to be independent of the grain
orientation. While the micrographs of specimen 4 and 6 reveal a distinct case-core
transition, a more gradual transition appears for specimen 2.
The case thicknesses determined from the Vickers hardness proΎυΒles (Figure 4.2) are
approximately 32 ┬╡m for specimen 2 and specimen 4 as well as 40 ┬╡m for specimen
6 and are in good accordance with the results obtained by light optical microscopy.
The near surface hardness decreases from 1140 HV for specimen 6 over 1040 HV for
specimen 4 down to 960 HV for specimen 2 and scales apparently with the alloysέΑβ
chromium content. It is therefore suggested that the carbon solubility in expanded
austenite is enhanced by an increasing chromium content.

4.2 X-ray DiΎυΑraction - Phase Analysis
DiΎυΑractograms in the as-carburized and as-received (reference) condition for speci-
mens 2, 4 and 6 are given in Figure 4.3. The results obtained during depth proΎυΒling
are omitted in Figure 4.3 for reasons of clarity and comprehensibility and are in-
stead presented in Figures B.3a, B.3b, B.4a and B.4b in the appendix. From the
obvious shift of the 111╬│ and 200╬│ to lower 2╬α values, it can be concluded that the
low temperature carburized specimens consist of expanded austenite. The magni-
tude of the peak shift decreases in the following order; specimen 6, specimen 4 and
specimen 2. It is assumed that the observed diΎυΑerence in the peak shift is caused by
the stainless steelsέΑβ chromium content, such that a higher chromium content results
in a higher solubility of carbon, causing more pronounced lattice expansion and
associated larger compressive residual stresses.

The presence of additional peaks in the diΎυΑractogram of specimen 2, result from

carbide peak is hidden under the 111 peak of expanded austenite.
The 111 peak of expanded austenite of specimen 4 and 6 are not only well-deΎυΒned,
but feature a tail on their high 2╬α side which is attributed to the carbon concen-
tration proΎυΒle in the layer of expanded austenite. Whether this tail is present in
specimen 2 or not, cannot be stated unambiguously, since it could be hidden un-
der the additional peaks originating from the presence of H├νgg carbide. No H├νgg
carbide was identiΎυΒed for specimens 4 and 6.
4.3 Glow Discharge Optical Emission Spectroscopy
Carbon concentration proΎυΒles for specimen 2, 4 and 6 obtained by GDOES as well
as the corresponding crater proΎυΒles are presented in Figure 4.4a and Figures 4.4b
to 4.4d.
Near-surface carbon contents are as high as approximately 26 at% for
specimen 6, 21 at% for specimen 4 and around 15 έΙΤ16 at% in case of specimen
2, corroborating that the Cr-content is indeed the governing factor for the carbon
solubility in expanded austenite as suggested by the presented hardness proΎυΒles in
Figure 4.2 and the results obtained by XRD (Figure 4.3).
Comparing the carbon concentration proΎυΒles with the respective hardness proΎυΒles,
it is noticed that the concentration proΎυΒles of all three specimens are shallower than
predicted by the hardness proΎυΒles. The reason behind this eΎυΑect is related to the
measurement principle of GDOES, which causes a smearing of the concentration
proΎυΒles and thus shallower concentration gradients than expected. Since each data
point along the concentration proΎυΒle represents an eΎυΑective depth-averaged value
that corresponds to the crater bottomέΑβs curvature, this smearing is intensiΎυΒed if
the sputtering rate is not constant over the anodeέΑβs surface area, resulting in ei-
ther convex or concave crater bottoms. DiΎυΑerent sputter rates in ╬│C and ╬│ as well
as the sputter rateέΑβs dependence on the carbon content will furthermore intensify
the smearing. The crater proΎυΒles of the measurements for specimen 2, 4 and 6 in
Figures 4.4b to 4.4d are reasonably ΎυΓat. The slightly concave shape of the crater
bottoms of specimen 2 and 6 is assumed to be caused by averaging several parallel
line proΎυΒles of the crater, while not taking the craterέΑβs circular shape into account.
An impairment of the presented results due to uneven sputtering is considered to
be negligible.
The carbon proΎυΒles of specimen 4 and 6 indicate thicknesses of the expanded austen-
ite layer of 32.5 ┬╡m and 38 ┬╡m, respectively. This corresponds well with the values
obtained by light optical microscopy and hardness tests. In case of specimen 2, the
determined depths of the expanded austenite case by GDOES (40 ┬╡m) on the one
hand and LOM and hardness testing (30 ┬╡m) on the other hand, do not match. It
is considered likely that the high carbon concentrations (> 16 at%) down to a depth
of approximately 1.5-2 ╬╝m are associated with the presence of H├νgg carbide on the
30
==PAGE:57==
4 Results and Interpretation
surface of specimen 2. This coincides with the absence of H├νgg carbide peaks in
the diΎυΑractogram of specimen 2 at 3 ╬╝m below the original surface (Figure B.3a).
Since the specimens used for GDOES were only ground up to 1000 grit, some H├νgg
carbide, formed in between the roughness peaks, will be measured at a later point
in time and associated to larger depths. Hence, the carbon concentration proΎυΒle
will be smeared out and is probably slightly overestimated within the ΎυΒrst couple of
microns. Roughness measurements of the specimens are still pending. Whether the
diΎυΑerence in case thickness of specimen 2 is actually related to the surface roughness
and the present H├νgg Carbide, can for the moment not be stated.

Although the linear regressionέΑβs R2 values shown in Figure 4.5d are higher than
0.9 for the majority of the data sets in Figures 4.5a to 4.5c, especially the d111 vs.
sin2╧Ι relations of specimen 4 and 6 appear to be non-linear. Despite being small,
the observed non-linearity is too consistent to be caused by statistical errors and
becomes more pronounced, particularly in specimen 4, deeper down in the layer of
expanded austenite. As mentioned in Section 2.4.3 non-linear sin2╧ΙέΙΤplots can be
caused by steep stress gradients, due to the presence of shear stresses or as a result
from pronounced crystallographic texture.
An impairment of the measurement results due to the present stress gradients can
be excluded, since the X-raysέΑβ penetration depth is kept constant at 0.5 ╬╝m. This
implies that the curved d111 vs. sin2╧Ι relations result either from shear stresses or
arise due to texture.
Sin2╧Ι-plots for specimens 4 and 6 measured in +╧Ι as well as in έΙΤ╧Ι direction are
shown in Figures 4.6a and 4.6b. Measurements in έΙΤ╧Ι direction were performed by
rotating the specimens around the diΎυΑractometers ╧ΧέΙΤaxis by 180έΩο. Even though
the d111 vs. sin2╧Ι relations determined in the έΙΤ╧Ι direction tend to exhibit higher
d-spacingέΑβs, the curvature remains convex. The results for specimen 2, shown in
Appendix B.4 in the appendix, are in good agreement with those of specimen 4 and
6. Hence, there is no evidence for the presence of shear stresses. Otherwise, the
d111 vs. sin2╧Ι relations for +╧Ι and έΙΤ╧Ι would form an ellipsoid shape. Since this is
not the case, the non-linearity of the sin2╧Ι-plots must have an other cause.

than those in the ISO-direction, which implies that the stress state in the expanded
austenite zone is non-rotationally symmetric. The diΎυΑerences in slope amount to
0.005 ├Ζ for specimen 6 and 0.01843 ├Ζ for specimen 4.

==PAGE:61==
4 Results and Interpretation
As mentioned in Section 2.4.5, the diΎυΑraction vector and hence the direction in
which the lattice strains are determined rotate as a consequence of the diΎυΑerent ap-
plied ╧Ι angles (diΎυΑerent combinations of grazing incidence angle ╬▒G and ╧Θ angle).
In case of a non-rotationally symmetric stress state, this implies that each lattice
spacing in single dhkl vs. sin2 ╧Ι relation corresponds to a certain stress state, as the
stress is direction dependent. Thus, the observed non linear sin2╧Ι-plots might result
from the presence of a crystallographic texture and the consequent non-rotationally
symmetric stress state.
Although a non-rotationally symmetric stress state is not an unambiguous indi-
cation for the presence of a crystallographic texture, it is nonetheless considered
likely that the specimens exhibit a preferred orientation due to hot rolling, which
causes the observed non-linear sin2╧Ι-plots. This assumption is supported by the
relative intensity ratios of the d111 vs. sin2╧Ι relations given in Figures 4.8a to 4.8f.
The relative intensities of the carburized specimens exhibit a systematic variation
which is independent of the depth in the expanded austenite case. Whereas the
relative intensity curves for specimen 4 vary to a greater extend than those of spec-
imen 6, which should indicate a more pronounced crystallographic texture and is in
excellent agreement with the results presented for the variation of the sin2╧Ι-plots
along and perpendicular to the ISO-direction in Figures 4.7a and 4.7b. The results
for specimen 2 are shown in Figures B.10a and B.10b in the appendix and are in
accordance with the drawn conclusions, in terms of less relative intensity variation
results in less curved d111 vs. sin2╧Ι relations. It should be noted that the measure-
ments directly on the surface of specimens 4 and 6 are not included, since the peak
intensity in terms of the integrated peak area could not be determined consistently
due to the tail on the high 2╬α side.

==PAGE:63==
4 Results and Interpretation
Apart from the curvature, the sin2╧ΙέΙΤplots in Figures 4.5a to 4.5c exhibit that the
d111 vs. sin2╧Ι relations for the low temperature carburized specimens are shifted to
higher lattice spacingέΑβs compared to those of the reference specimens, even though
the thickness of the removed material layer indicates that the last measurements
were performed close to the case core transition (specimen 2) or already in the bulk
material underneath the zone of expanded austenite (specimens 4 and 6). Possible
causes for the deviations between the determined and the expected lattice spacings
were seen in the three following scenarios: (a) the specimens were ΎυΒrst high temper-
ature solution nitrided before being low temperature carburized, (b) the reference
specimens were interchanged and do not correspond to the chemical composition
of the carburized specimens, (c) the hardness indents were placed to close to the
edges of the specimens and were aΎυΑected by edge rounding so that the thickness
of the eΎυΑectively removed material layer in the center of the specimens is less than
what is indicated by tracing the hardness indents diagonals.
In case that the specimens were, prior to Kolsterising, high temperature solution
nitrided, it would be expected that the hardness continues to decrease in the austen-
ite underneath the layer of carbon expanded austenite due to the imposed nitrogen
concentration proΎυΒle. Since the hardness proΎυΒles of specimens 2, 4 and 6 in Fig-
ure 4.2 do not exhibit such a trend, the option of a HTSN treatment can be ruled
out.

4 Results and Interpretation
ference in lattice spacings between the as-received and the re-polished reference
specimens is considered to lie within the measurement techniqueέΑβs accuracy.
ProΎυΒle measurements of specimens 2, 4 and 6 are given in Figures 4.10a to 4.10c
and reveal that the hardness indents indicated by the grey dashed lines are indeed
aΎυΑected by edge rounding. The determined thickness of the removed layer is hence

carburized specimens. It is considered likely that the surface curvature was steadily
increasing with ongoing layer removal steps, but a correction of the sin2╧ΙέΙΤplots for
this eΎυΑect is deemed unreasonable. The results presented in the following sections
are therefore given as function of the determined distance from the original surface,
bearing in mind that these values are overestimated and that the actual distance
from the original surface is shifted to lower values.

4.4.1 Compositional Analysis
The lattice parameters for specimens 2, 4 and 6, calculated based on the strain-
free lattice spacing from the sin2╧ΙέΙΤplots in Figures 4.5a to 4.5c, are shown in
Figure 4.11a. Specimen 6 exhibits with 5.2 % the highest lattice expansion, followed
by specimen 4 (4.7 %) and specimen 2 (2.6 %). The stated lattice expansion of
specimen 2 is determined by comparing the referenceέΑβs lattice parameter with the
one determined at 3 ╬╝m.
The varying degree of lattice expansion conΎυΒrms the
results of hardness testing and GDOES insofar that specimen 6 exhibits the highest
carbon uptake, implying that the carbon content scales indeed with the Cr-content.

4 Results and Interpretation
Comparing the carbon concentrations proΎυΒles determined by XRD, applying the
approximation formula proposed by Hummelsh├╕j et al. [34] and extrapolating it to
yC = 0.25, with the concentrations proΎυΒles obtained by GDOES, as shown in Fig-
ures 4.11b to 4.11d, reveals that the results from XRD ΎυΒt rather well to the GDOES
results within the ΎυΒrst 15 έΙΤ20 ╬╝m for specimens 4 and 6 and up to 25 ┬╡m for spec-
imen 2 of the expanded austenite case. The impairment of the depth measurement
by edge rounding becomes more apparent for depths larger than 20 ╬╝m, leading to
erroneous carbon concentration proΎυΒles. Although the XRD measurements do not
allow the construction of quantitative carbon concentration proΎυΒles throughout the
entire layer of expanded austenite, they still provide valid qualitative information
concerning the carbon content in the ΎυΒrst couple of micrometers.
4.4.2 Stress Analysis
Figures 4.12a to 4.12c display the slopes of the sin2╧ΙέΙΤplots shown in Figures 4.5a
to 4.5c. The error bars correspond to the uncertainties from the linear regression
analysis. In accordance with the GDOES and hardness results, specimen 6 exhibits
steeper slopes than specimens 4 and 2 as a consequence of the higher carbon up-
take. Whereas the slopes of specimen 4 and 6 decrease with the distance from the
specimensέΑβ surface alongside the declining the carbon concentration proΎυΒles, the re-
sults for specimen 2 show increasingly steeper slopes further down in the expanded
austenite. It is considered reasonable, that the formation of H├νgg carbide in the
near-surface area results will cause a certain amount of stress relaxation. However,
stress relaxation at the surface is unlikely to aΎυΑect the slopes up to several microm-
eters, and the actual cause for the observed trend in έΙΗd111/ sin2 ╧Ι for specimen 2
has so far not been identiΎυΒed.

Misorientation proΎυΒles for the grains in Section 4.5, determined along the black
arrows (Section 4.5) as point to reference, are shown in Figure 4.14a. The lattice
rotationέΑβs magnitude is comparable to the accuracy of the orientation determination
of 2έΩο. Plastic deformation due to the residual stresses in specimen 2 is hence consid-
ered unlikely, especially since the image quality map of specimen 2 (Figure 4.13a)
does not reveal signs of plastic deformation in the case of expanded austenite, such
as slip lines or deformation twins. The nearly constant misorientation proΎυΒles for
grains in the austenitic bulk (Figure B.11a) given in Figure 4.14b, illustrate that
the measurements were not impaired by the manual specimen preparation.

The OIM and IPF of specimen 4, depicted in Figures 4.15a and 4.15b, exhibit
curved grain and twin boundaries in the outer 10 - 15 ┬╡m of the expanded austenite
layer. It is assumed that the curvature is caused by drift. Intriguingly, the depth
up to which curved twin boundaries are observed coincides with the thickness of
the layer of ferromagnetic expanded austenite, see for further information Chap-
ter 5. Whether, and if yes to what extent, this drift inΎυΓuences the misorientation
measurements is not known.
Despite the image quality map of specimen 4 (Figure 4.15a) reveals dark linear
features in the near-surface area which could be slip lines and hence indicate that the
residual stresses were at least partially plastically accommodated, the determined
misorientation proΎυΒles in Figure 4.15i exhibit no clear indication of lattice rotation.
Nevertheless, regions with a high density of slip lines exhibit a distinctly lower
indexing rate as other areas in the specimen, which suggests that the depicted
misorientation proΎυΒles of grain 1 and 3 are likely underestimated. On the contrary,
the low misorientation in grain 2 coincides with its crystallographic orientation being
close to <100>, as <100> is one of the two stable crystallographic orientations in
a compressive stress state and correspondingly oriented grains are not expected to
rotate.

deeper down in the bulk towards the case/core transition. Whether this indicates an
impairment of the EBSD results is not known. Nevertheless, the observed lattice
rotations are considered to be signiΎυΒcant, even if the polishing did inΎυΓuence the
lattice rotation.
46
==PAGE:73==
5 Discussion
Carbon concentration proΎυΒles measured by GDOES, shown in Section 4.3, exhibit
an increase in carbon solubility from specimen 2 over specimen 4 to specimen 6 and
are in excellent agreement with the observed increase in surface hardness upon low
temperature carburizing as well as with the determined lattice expansion by XRD.
The results suggest that the carbon solubility is governed by the alloysέΑβ chromium
content, in a way that higher chromium contents enhance the carbon uptake of
austenitic stainless steels.
This is consistent with observations for nitrogen ex-
panded austenite, which indicate a relation between the content of strong nitride
forming elements, such as Cr, V, Nb [32] as well as Ti [33], and an enhanced nitro-
gen solubility.
Specimen 2 exhibits a near-surface carbon content of 15-16 at%, which is consider-
ably higher than commonly reported carbon concentrations in AISI 316L of up to
11.7 at% [17]. The presence of H├νgg-carbide at the surface of specimen 2 indicates
that the austeniteέΑβs carbon solubility was exceeded during Kolsterising. Given that
the maximum solubility of AISI 316 is έΚΙ17 at% [30], it is considered reasonable
that the near-surface carbon concentration in the expanded austenite of specimen
2 is nearly as high as 17 at%. The diΎυΑractograms of specimen 4 and 6 did not re-
veal additional diΎυΑraction peaks besides those of expanded austenite. It is assumed
that the absence of H├νgg-carbide in specimens 4 and 6 is related to the substan-
tial higher Ni-content. Recent ΎυΒndings indicate that the chemical composition of
H├νgg-Carbide, with regards to the substitutional elements, is similar to the stain-
less steels chemical composition. Therefore, higher nickel contents are considered to
impair the formation of H├νgg-Carbide due to its low aΎυΔnity to carbon. An alterna-
tive reasoning for the absence of H├νgg carbide in specimens 4 and 6, is seen in the
fact that Fe5C2 is metastable with respect to graphite. Chromium carbides on the
other hand are stable. Increasing the chromium content of austenitic stainless steel,
by replacing Fe with Cr, will result in an enhanced carbon solubility in expanded
austenite and concurrently in a decrease in the Fe:C ratio, which is tantamount
with a reduced driving force for the formation of Fe5C2. Comparing the specimens
Cr:C, which are not given here, with each other might indicate whether the absence
of H├νgg carbide is indeed related to a decrease in driving as the chromium content
increases.
Comparing the carbon concentrations determined by GDOES and XRD in Fig-
ures 4.11b to 4.11d reveals that the results are in good accordance within the ΎυΒrst
15 ╬╝m of the expanded austenite zone. The obvious mismatch for depths > 15 ╬╝m is
presumably an outcome of the overestimated thickness of the removed sublayers due
to the occurrence of edge rounding. Furthermore, the increase in curvature of the
47
==PAGE:74==
5 Discussion
sin2╧ΙέΙΤplots with depth shifts the strain-free lattice spacing to higher values and is
seen as an additional reason for the observed discrepancy of the GDOES and XRD
results. The carbon concentrations proΎυΒles determined by XRD provide hence solely
a qualitative picture of the compositional gradient in expanded austenite. Never-
theless, it can be concluded that extrapolating the lattice parameter dependence for
strain-free carbon expanded austenite on carbon content by Hummelsh├╕j et al. [34]
to yC = 0.25 results in realistic carbon contents. The reasoning behind extrapo-
lating the formula to the chemical composition of ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C is that the strain-free
lattice parameter of specimens 4 and 6 exceeds the 3.740 - 3.768 ├Ζ calculated for
╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C by density functional theory (DFT) [36,38]. Furthermore, the discrepancy
between the lattice parameter calculated by Bianc├κ et al. (3.768 ├Ζ) [38] and the
value predicted by the approximation formula (3.75 ├Ζ) is negligible.
Micrographs acquired by magnetic force microscopy of the cross section of specimens
2, 4 and 6 are shown in Figures 5.1a to 5.1c1.
It is apparent that specimen 2
remains paramagnetic, which is in agreement with the limited lattice expansion
of approximately 2.6 %.
Intriguingly, the GDOES carbon concentration proΎυΒle
indicates that specimen 2 should become ferromagnetic, since the threshold of έΚΙ14
at% for the transition from para- to ferromagnetic in nitrogen expanded austenite
is exceeded [22]. The MFM result is however conΎυΒrmed by measurements that were
conducted with a FERITSCOPE on the as-carburized surface of specimen 2, see
hereto Table 5.1.

==PAGE:75==
5 Discussion
As opposed to specimen 2, the MFM micrographs of specimens 4 and 6 indicate
ferromagnetic domains in the near-surface region. While ferromagnetism in speci-
men 6 is, except for some isolated linear features, limited to the outermost layers,
specimen 4 exhibits ferromagnetic behaviour down to a depth of approximately
12 ╬╝m, corresponding to a carbon content of approximately 14.5 at%. The fer-
romagnetic domains in specimen 4 seem to coincide with linear features that are
inclined to specimens surface, and are especially pronounced at crossings of such
lines. Measurements conducted with the FERITSCOPE conΎυΒrm the MFM results
insofar that, contrary to expectations, the lattice expansions is not the governing
factor for the transition from para- to ferromagnetism. The absence of diΎυΑraction
peaks corresponding to body centered cubic (bcc) phase constituents in the diΎυΑrac-
togram of specimen 4 implies that the magnetic contrast int the MFM micrographs
does not arise from the presence of ferrite or martensite.
The diΎυΑerence in the nickel content of specimens 4 and 6 is about 6 wt%, in favour
of specimen 4, and it is suggested that the diΎυΑerence in the magnetic properties is
related to the content of inherently magnetic nickel. Furthermore, this could also
explain the absence of ferromagnetic domains in specimen 2, even though its carbon
content is higher than the threshold of approximately 14 at% of interstitial elements
as applies for nitrogen stabilized expanded austenite. Preliminary results for low
temperature carburized AISI 310 MoLN, containing 24.68 wt% Cr and 24.34 wt%
Ni, support the assumption of Nickel as a dominant factor in the transition from
para- to ferromagnetism in carbon expanded austenite. Since the lattice expansion
for AISI 310 MoLN is comparable to the one for specimen 6, i.e. 4.6 % to 5.2 %, and
the FERITSCOPE measurements indicate a similar level of magnetism (Fe%=2.90)
for 310 MoLN as for specimen 4 (Fe%=2.90), which excludes carbon content as the
dominant factor. A clearer assessment of the inΎυΓuence of carbon and the alloysέΑβ
chemical composition on the magnetic properties of expanded austenite requires
further investigations.
Even though carbon will contribute considerably to solid solution strengthening,
the residual stresses calculated on the basis of single crystal elastic constants of a
Fe-18wt%Cr-12wt%Ni alloy surpass physically reasonable values, e.g. 5 GPa, and
are hence suspected to be overestimated. It is considered that the occurring plastic
deformation (section 4.5) as well as the mismatch in the chemical composition be-
tween the Fe-18wt%Cr-12wt%Ni alloyέΑβs composition and the specimensέΑβ chemical
composition, especially the carbon content, are root causes for the overestimated
residual stresses. Given that the lattice parameters in the expanded austenite layer
of specimens 4 and 6 surpass the theoretical lattice parameter of ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C, it is
suggested to calculate the XECs of ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C from single crystal elastic constants
by applying the Voigt-Reuss mean average (Hill-Neerfeld approximation). The ob-
tained XECs are S
111F e4C
1
= έΙΤ1.91 ┬╖ 10έΙΤ6 MPa and 1
2S
111F e4C
2
= 7.21 ┬╖ 10έΙΤ6 MPa.
Results for the evaluation of the sin2╧ΙέΙΤplots for specimens 4 and 6, using the afore-
mentioned XECs for ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C, are given in Figures 5.2a and 5.2b together with the
results for specimen 2 (Fe-18wt%Cr-12wt%Ni XECs). It is obvious that the choice
49
==PAGE:76==
5 Discussion
of the XECs has a pronounced inΎυΓuence on the magnitude of the residual stresses
calculated from the lattice strains, whereas the lattice parameter is less sensitive
with regards to the chosen XECs. For Specimen 6, the carbon concentration proΎυΒles
based on the Fe-18wt%Cr-12wt%Ni XECs appear to be in better agreement with
the GDOES results, while for specimen 4 the X-ray elastic constants of ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C
appear a more appropriate assumption.
Although the elastic modulusέΑβ anisotropy of ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C (E200
Fe4C > E111
Fe4C) corresponds
to the reversed anisotropy in expanded austenite [47, 58], the stresses determined
based on the ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C XECs are still unreasonably high. In light of the observed
indications for plastic deformation in the expanded austenite layer of specimen 6
and likely also in specimen 4, the aforementioned results suggest that a mere adap-
tation of the XECs for carbon expanded austenite with regards to the chemical
composition is not suΎυΔcient.
Furthermore, the pronounced variation in peak intensity for the diΎυΑerent sin2╧ΙέΙΤ
values (Figures 4.8a, 4.8c and 4.8e) as well as the indications for a non-ideal ro-
tationally symmetric stress state (Figure 4.7a) in specimen 4 are indicative that
the Hill GIM is not applicable, since the material is not quasi-isotropic. The use
of X-ray stress factors, taking the orientation distribution of the crystallites into
account, seems more appropriate, but this is a very elaborate technique and far
beyond the scope of the present project.
Despite the inΎυΓuence of edge rounding, as discussed in Section 2.5.1 and the issues
related to the choice of reasonable XECs, the residual stress over depth proΎυΒles
present a strictly qualitative corroboration that the compressive residual stress in
carbon expanded austenite is linked to the compositional gradient, such that higher
carbon concentrations result in higher compressive residual stresses. Similar ΎυΒnd-
ings were presented by Christiansen & Somers for carbon expanded austenite [13]
and by Somers et al. for nitrogen expanded austenite [21].
The misorientation proΎυΒles for specimen 2 in Figure 4.14a did not exhibit a signif-
icant degree of lattice rotation. In combination with the absence of shear bands
in the image quality maps, it is reasoned that plastic accommodation of the com-
pressive residual stresses did not occur in specimen 2. On the contrary Peng et al.
saw that for low-temperature gas carburized AISI 316L it was necessary to include
plastic deformation to obtain a satisfactory correspondence between experimental
and predicted stress distributions [17]. Also Christiansen & Somers suggested the
occurrence of plastic deformation to explain the discrepancy between stresses pre-
dicted from the lattice expansion and the assessed stress values [13]. The residual
stresses in specimen 2 are lower than the usually reported residual stresses for car-
bon expanded austenite on austenitic stainless with a Cr-content of approx. 18
wt% [17,61] and behave atypically as they increase with depth. It is therefore sug-
gested that the precipitation of H├νgg carbides will occur, such that the associated
volume change complies with the stress distribution already present and, accord-
ingly, partially release the stresses induced by the carbon proΎυΒle.

Contrary to specimen 2, the misorientation proΎυΒles determined for specimen 6 are
considered signiΎυΒcant and reach misorientation values as high as 9έΩο. The occurrence
of lattice rotation is in agreement with the observation of dark linear features in the
image quality maps, which are assumed to be shear bands and hence indicate the
occurrence of plastic deformation. Whether the degree of lattice rotation depends
on the crystallographic orientation of the grains, as proven by Templier et al. and
others [20,21], can not be stated, since it was not investigated. Here, solely grain 1
extends all the way from the specimenέΑβs surface down to the underlying austenite
and the misorientation proΎυΒles for grains 2 and 3 reΎυΓect only the ΎυΒrst 20 ╬╝m of ex-
panded austenite. Furthermore, the partial plastic accommodation of the residual
stress in specimen 6 is supported by the very high elastic strains; thus the likelihood
that plastic deformation has occurred is enhanced.
51
==PAGE:78==
5 Discussion
Although the image quality map of specimen 4 (Figure 4.15a) indicates the presence
of shear bands and the sin2╧Ι-plots slopes are comparable to those of specimen 6,
the corresponding misorientation proΎυΒles exhibit lattice rotations of less than 3έΩο. It
is suggested that the small lattice rotations in case of grain 1 and 3 are linked to the
considerable decrease in the indexing rate close to the surface, whereas specimen
2 is oriented close to the stable 100 direction. Furthermore, it is not known how
the apparent drift inΎυΓuences the misorientation proΎυΒles. Intriguingly, the depth
up to which drift is observed coincides with the thickness of the layer exhibiting
ferromagnetism. This suggests that drift is caused by an interaction of the electron
beam and the weak magnetic ΎυΒeld of the sample.
52
==PAGE:79==
6 Conclusion
The presented results indicate that the response of austenitic stainless steel on low-
temperature carburizing depends signiΎυΒcantly on the alloysέΑβ chromium and nickel
equivalent. Higher Cr-equivalents, decreasing from specimen 6 (1.4565) over speci-
men 4 (AISI 904L) to specimen 2 (AISI 316L), are suggested to enhance the carbon
solubility in expanded austenite and result in more pronounced lattice expansion.
The carbon content in specimens 4 and 6 exceeds the reported solubility limit of
AISI 316 by far, while H├νgg carbide is formed in the near-surface region of specimen
2, as consequence of exceeding the aforementioned solubility limit. Compared to
low-temperature carburized AISI 316 L, specimens 4 and 6 exhibit high compressive
residual stress in the layer of expanded austenite and a dramatic increase of up to
1200 HV in surface hardness as a result of the carbon-induced lattice expansion.
For the ΎυΒrst time, it was shown that the residual stresses in carbon expanded austen-
ite are partly plastically accommodated by lattice rotation. Furthermore, the resid-
ual stress proΎυΒles determined by X-ray diΎυΑraction depend signiΎυΒcantly on the chosen
X-ray elastic constants and it was proven that neither the X-ray elastic constants
of a Fe-18wt%Cr-12wt%Ni alloy nor the ΎυΒrst principle elastic constants of ╬│έΑ▓έΙΤFe4C
result in reasonable elastic stress values. It is reasoned that the X-ray elastic con-
stants for expanded austenite have to consider the eΎυΑect of plastic deformation as
well the inΎυΓuence of high carbon contents.
Micrographs obtained by magnetic force microscopy and measurements with a
FERITSCOPE R
έΔζelucidate that low-temperature carburized austenitic stainless steels
exhibit a transition from para- to ferromagnetism at a carbon content of approxi-
mately 14.5 at%, similar to low temperature nitrided austenitic stainless steel. Con-
trary to observations for nitrogen expanded austenite, the occurrence of ferromag-
netism in carbon expanded austenite cannot solely be attributed to the interstitially-
induced lattice expansion, but depends predominantly on the alloyέΑβs Ni-equivalent.
It is considered recommendable to investigate the expanded austenite zone by trans-
mission electron microscopy to obtain information about the crystallography of the
linear features observed in the magnetic force micrographs on a sub-micron level.
Concerning the residual stress analysis by X-ray diΎυΑraction, it is seen as advan-
tageous to perform the measurements on specimens that were annealed prior to
low-temperature carburizing. Furthermore, the determination of the removed sub-
layer thickness has to be improved in order to avoid inΎυΓuences by edge rounding or
at least enable the correction for it.
